    <section id="abstract" class="introductory"><h2>Abstract</h2>
A <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-credential-1">verifiable credential</a> is a specific way to express a set of <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-claims" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-claims-1">claims</a>
made by an <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-issuers" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-issuers-1">issuer</a>, such as a driver's license or an education certificate. This
specification describes the extensible data model for <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-credential-2">verifiable credentials</a>,
the exchange of these credentials that is composed of <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-issuers" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-issuers-2">issuers</a>, <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-holders" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-holders-1">holders</a>,
and <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifier" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifier-1">verifiers</a>. This document also covers a variety of security, privacy,
      document at the time of its publication. A list of current <abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr>
      <a href="https://www.w3.org/TR/"><abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr> standards and drafts index</a> at
      <abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr> recommends the wide deployment of this specification as a standard for
      A <abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr> Recommendation is a specification that, after extensive
      <abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr> and its Members, and
        <a href="https://www.w3.org/policies/patent-policy/"><abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr> Patent
                <abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr> maintains a
          <a href="https://www.w3.org/policies/patent-policy/#sec-Disclosure">section 6 of the <abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr> Patent Policy</a>.
                      <a id="w3c_process_revision" href="https://www.w3.org/policies/process/20231103/">03 November 2023 <abbr title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</abbr> Process Document</a>.

    <section class="informative" id="introduction"><div class="header-wrapper"><h2 id="x1-introduction"><bdi class="secno">1. </bdi>Introduction</h2><a class="self-link" href="#introduction" aria-label="Permalink for Section 1."></a></div><p><em>This section is non-normative.</em></p>
<a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-1">Credentials</a> are integral to our daily lives: driver's licenses confirm
expressing these sorts of <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-2">credentials</a> on the Web in a way that is
<a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-3">credentials</a> provide benefits to us when used in the physical world, but
data, financial account details, and other third-party-<a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verify" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verify-1">verified</a>
expressing digital <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-4">credentials</a> on the Web hinders our ability to receive
the same benefits from them that physical <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-5">credentials</a> provide in the
<a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-credential-3">verifiable credentials</a>, the following sections provide an overview of:
The components that constitute a <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-credential-4">verifiable credential</a>
The components that constitute a <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-presentation" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-presentation-1">verifiable presentation</a>
An ecosystem where <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-credential-5">verifiable credentials</a>
and <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-presentation" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-presentation-2">verifiable presentations</a> are useful
in <cite><a data-matched-text="[[[VC-USE-CASES]]]" href="https://www.w3.org/TR/vc-use-cases/">Verifiable Credentials Use Cases</a></cite> [<cite><a class="bibref" data-link-type="biblio" href="#bib-vc-use-cases" title="Verifiable Credentials Use Cases">VC-USE-CASES</a></cite>].
In the physical world, a <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-6">credential</a> might consist of:
Information related to identifying the <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-subjects" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-subjects-1">subject</a> of the <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-7">credential</a>
Information related to the type of <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-8">credential</a> (for example, a
the issuing authority about the <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-subjects" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-subjects-2">subject</a> (for example, nationality,
Evidence by which a <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-subjects" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-subjects-3">subject</a> was demonstrated to have satisfied the
qualifications required for issuance of the <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-9">credential</a> (for example,
A <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-credential-6">verifiable credential</a> can represent all the same information that a
physical <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-credential-10">credential</a> represents. Adding technologies such as
digital signatures can make <a data-link-type="dfn|abstract-op" href="#dfn-verifiable-credential" class="internalDFN" id="ref-for-dfn-verifiable-credential-7">verifiable credentials</a> more tamper-evident and

## Classification
- On-chain identity/KYC: DID, Verifiable Credentials, ERC-1400, ERC-3643
- Regulation monitoring/reporting: MiCA, FATF RBA for VAs/VASPs, FinCEN Travel Rule, TRISA

--- FILE: related_work/categorization.md ---
# Related Work Categorization for APOL

## Category 1: On-Chain Identity & KYC Standards
### W3C DID Core (did-core.html)
- Decentralized identifier standard for verifiable digital identity
- Enables controller-based identity without centralized registries
- **Gap vs APOL**: DIDs provide identity infrastructure but lack policy enforcement mechanisms

### W3C Verifiable Credentials (vc-data-model.html)
- Privacy-preserving credential model with selective disclosure
- Standardizes claims about subjects with cryptographic verification
- **Gap vs APOL**: VCs handle credential verification but don't enforce on-chain compliance rules

### Ethereum Attestation Service (eas-docs.html)
- On-chain attestation infrastructure for verifiable claims
- Composable attestation schemas with on-chain/off-chain storage
- **Gap vs APOL**: EAS provides attestation primitives but lacks regulatory policy language and enforcement automation

## Category 2: Token-Level Compliance & Regulatory Enforcement
### EIP-3643 T-REX (eip-3643.html)
- Permissioned token standard with compliance rules embedded in smart contracts
- Identity registry + compliance rules for regulated securities
- **Gap vs APOL**: Token-specific compliance vs. APOL's protocol-level policy enforcement across diverse DeFi interactions

### EIP-1400 Security Token Standard (eip-1400.html)
- Partially fungible token standard with document management and transfer restrictions
- Operator-based controls for regulated asset transfers
- **Gap vs APOL**: Focuses on asset tokenization compliance, not general DeFi protocol policy enforcement

## Category 3: DeFi Policy & Risk Management
- **TO COLLECT**: On-chain policy enforcement frameworks, DeFi regulation monitoring systems, automated compliance tools for lending/DEX protocols

## Category 4: Privacy-Preserving Compliance
- **OVERLAP**: W3C VCs, potential ZK-proof-based KYC systems
- **TO COLLECT**: zk-KYC solutions, privacy-preserving regulatory reporting

## Key Insights:
1. **Identity Layer**: W3C standards + EAS provide building blocks APOL can leverage
2. **Token Compliance**: EIP-3643/1400 solve asset-level rules; APOL targets protocol interaction policies
3. **Missing Gap**: No existing work combines expressive policy language + automated enforcement + privacy preservation for general DeFi protocols

--- END OF FILE ---

We categorize related work into two main areas: identity and credential frameworks, and token compliance standards.\n\n\\subsection{Identity and Credential Frameworks}\n\nDecentralized identity systems have emerged as critical infrastructure for on-chain regulation. The W3C Verifiable Credentials Data Model~\\cite{vc-data-model} provides a standard format for cryptographically secure, privacy-respecting credentials that can be verified without relying on centralized authorities. Decentralized Identifiers (DIDs)~\\cite{did-core} complement this by enabling persistent, cryptographically verifiable identifiers controlled by their subjects rather than centralized registries.\n\nThe Ethereum Attestation Service (EAS)~\\cite{eas-docs} offers a flexible on-chain attestation framework that allows any entity to make verifiable claims about addresses, enabling reputation systems and compliance verification. APOL leverages these standards for representing regulatory policies and user credentials, extending them with automated enforcement mechanisms.\n\n\\subsection{Token Compliance Standards}\n\nSeveral Ethereum Improvement Proposals (EIPs) address the need for compliant security tokens. EIP-3643~\\cite{eip-3643} (T-REX) defines a standard for permissioned tokens with built-in compliance rules, enabling transfer restrictions based on investor identity and jurisdiction. EIP-1400~\\cite{eip-1400} provides a security token standard with advanced features like tranches, controller operations, and document management.\n\nWhile these standards focus on security token issuance and transfer compliance, APOL addresses the broader challenge of applying arbitrary regulatory policies to existing DeFi protocols without requiring protocol modifications. Our attestation-based approach enables flexible policy enforcement that can adapt to diverse regulatory requirements across different jurisdictions.\n\n% TODO: Add citations for papers on DeFi governance, on-chain regulation monitoring, and privacy-preserving compliance\n'''; tex_path = Path('relatedwork.tex'); tex_path.write_text(content); print(f'✓ Written {len(content)} chars to {tex_path}'); print('\n--- PREVIEW (first 500 chars) ---'); print(content[:500]); print('\n--- END PREVIEW ---'); print('\nNOTE: BibTeX entries needed for: vc-data-model, did-core, eas-docs, eip-3643, eip-1400')"

# Related Work Collection

## Classification
- On-chain identity/KYC: DID, Verifiable Credentials, ERC-1400, ERC-3643
- Regulation monitoring/reporting: MiCA, FATF RBA for VAs/VASPs, FinCEN Travel Rule, TRISA
- On-chain enforcement primitives: Ethereum Attestation Service (EAS)
- DeFi policy and risk governance: Aave v3, MakerDAO risk framework

## Sources (links)
- W3C, "Decentralized Identifiers (DID) Core 1.0" (2022): https://www.w3.org/TR/did-core/
- W3C, "Verifiable Credentials Data Model 1.1" (2022): https://www.w3.org/TR/vc-data-model/
- Ethereum Foundation, "ERC-1400: Security Token Standard" (2018): https://eips.ethereum.org/EIPS/eip-1400
- Ethereum Foundation, "ERC-3643: T-REX -- Security Token Standard" (2021): https://eips.ethereum.org/EIPS/eip-3643
- Ethereum Attestation Service, "EAS Documentation" (2023): https://docs.attest.sh/
- European Union, "Regulation (EU) 2023/1114 (MiCA)" (2023): https://eur-lex.europa.eu/eli/reg/2023/1114/oj
- FATF, "Guidance for a Risk-Based Approach to Virtual Assets and VASPs" (2019/2021): https://www.fatf-gafi.org/en/publications/Fatfrecommendations/Guidance-RBA-VA-VASPs.html
- FinCEN, "Funds Travel Rule and Funds Transmittal Regulations" (2019): https://www.fincen.gov/resources/statutes-and-regulations/guidance/funds-travel-rule

--- FILE: related_work/categorization.md ---
# Related Work Categorization for APOL

## Category 1: On-Chain Identity & KYC Standards
### W3C DID Core (did-core.html)
- Decentralized identifier standard for verifiable digital identity
- Enables controller-based identity without centralized registries
- **Gap vs APOL**: DIDs provide identity infrastructure but lack policy enforcement mechanisms

### W3C Verifiable Credentials (vc-data-model.html)
- Privacy-preserving credential model with selective disclosure
- Standardizes claims about subjects with cryptographic verification
- **Gap vs APOL**: VCs handle credential verification but don't enforce on-chain compliance rules

### Ethereum Attestation Service (eas-docs.html)
- On-chain attestation infrastructure for verifiable claims
- Composable attestation schemas with on-chain/off-chain storage
- **Gap vs APOL**: EAS provides attestation primitives but lacks regulatory policy language and enforcement automation

## Category 2: Token-Level Compliance & Regulatory Enforcement
### EIP-3643 T-REX (eip-3643.html)
- Permissioned token standard with compliance rules embedded in smart contracts
- Identity registry + compliance rules for regulated securities
- **Gap vs APOL**: Token-specific compliance vs. APOL's protocol-level policy enforcement across diverse DeFi interactions

### EIP-1400 Security Token Standard (eip-1400.html)
- Partially fungible token standard with document management and transfer restrictions
- Operator-based controls for regulated asset transfers
- **Gap vs APOL**: Focuses on asset tokenization compliance, not general DeFi protocol policy enforcement

## Category 3: DeFi Policy & Risk Management
- **TO COLLECT**: On-chain policy enforcement frameworks, DeFi regulation monitoring systems, automated compliance tools for lending/DEX protocols

## Category 4: Privacy-Preserving Compliance
- **OVERLAP**: W3C VCs, potential ZK-proof-based KYC systems
- **TO COLLECT**: zk-KYC solutions, privacy-preserving regulatory reporting

## Key Insights:
1. **Identity Layer**: W3C standards + EAS provide building blocks APOL can leverage
2. **Token Compliance**: EIP-3643/1400 solve asset-level rules; APOL targets protocol interaction policies
3. **Missing Gap**: No existing work combines expressive policy language + automated enforcement + privacy preservation for general DeFi protocols

--- END OF FILE ---

# Related Work Categorization for APOL

## Category 1: On-Chain Identity & KYC Standards
### W3C DID Core (did-core.html)
- Decentralized identifier standard for verifiable digital identity
- Enables controller-based identity without centralized registries
- **Gap vs APOL**: DIDs provide identity infrastructure but lack policy enforcement mechanisms

### W3C Verifiable Credentials (vc-data-model.html)
- Privacy-preserving credential model with selective disclosure
- Standardizes claims about subjects with cryptographic verification
- **Gap vs APOL**: VCs handle credential verification but don't enforce on-chain compliance rules

### Ethereum Attestation Service (eas-docs.html)
- On-chain attestation infrastructure for verifiable claims
- Composable attestation schemas with on-chain/off-chain storage
- **Gap vs APOL**: EAS provides attestation primitives but lacks regulatory policy language and enforcement automation

## Category 2: Token-Level Compliance & Regulatory Enforcement
### EIP-3643 T-REX (eip-3643.html)
- Permissioned token standard with compliance rules embedded in smart contracts
- Identity registry + compliance rules for regulated securities
- **Gap vs APOL**: Token-specific compliance vs. APOL's protocol-level policy enforcement across diverse DeFi interactions

### EIP-1400 Security Token Standard (eip-1400.html)
- Partially fungible token standard with document management and transfer restrictions
- Operator-based controls for regulated asset transfers
- **Gap vs APOL**: Focuses on asset tokenization compliance, not general DeFi protocol policy enforcement

## Category 3: DeFi Policy & Risk Management
- **TO COLLECT**: On-chain policy enforcement frameworks, DeFi regulation monitoring systems, automated compliance tools for lending/DEX protocols

## Category 4: Privacy-Preserving Compliance
- **OVERLAP**: W3C VCs, potential ZK-proof-based KYC systems
- **TO COLLECT**: zk-KYC solutions, privacy-preserving regulatory reporting

## Key Insights:
1. **Identity Layer**: W3C standards + EAS provide building blocks APOL can leverage
2. **Token Compliance**: EIP-3643/1400 solve asset-level rules; APOL targets protocol interaction policies
3. **Missing Gap**: No existing work combines expressive policy language + automated enforcement + privacy preservation for general DeFi protocols

--- END OF FILE ---

Here are the extracted points from the article:

Main points
- Blockchain KYC replaces repeated document submissions with a reusable, single verification shared across institutions.
- Only verification proofs (hashes + consent flags) go on-chain; sensitive documents stay encrypted off-chain.
- Smart contracts automate compliance checks and auditing, shrinking onboarding time.
- Permissioned networks (e.g., Hyperledger Fabric) are emphasized to meet compliance/privacy needs.
- Benefits: reduced redundancy/costs, stronger security, transparent audit trails, user control.
- Challenges: scalability, regulatory divergence, technical complexity, user adoption, interoperability.

Technical approaches described
- On-chain: immutable hashes of verified data; consent status recorded via smart contracts.
- Off-chain: encrypted document storage (e.g., IPFS or secure cloud).
- Permissioned blockchain architecture with vetted participants and private channels.
- Smart-contract driven consent management, including revocation transactions.
- Interoperability focus for new institutions joining existing networks.
- Potential future use of zero-knowledge proofs for selective disclosure (e.g., age/citizenship).

Key references
- The article does not list explicit references or citations.
- Mentioned technologies: Hyperledger Fabric, IPFS, smart contracts, cryptography, zero-knowledge proofs.

Banks and fintech firms often verify the same customer multiple times across institutions, increasing both risk and compliance fatigue. Blockchain offers a solution. With its decentralized, tamper-proof architecture, verified identities can be securely shared across trusted parties — reducing duplication and fraud. Many experts are now documenting this shift in detailed kyc blockchain whitepapers . These whitepapers outline how smart contracts, encrypted identity data, and distributed ledgers can streamline verification while improving compliance and trust. In this blog, we break down the key challenges in legacy KYC systems, how blockchain improves them, and what insights current whitepapers reveal for businesses looking to upgrade. In a Nutshell Traditional KYC systems are inefficient , involving repeated document submissions, high compliance costs, and poor user experiences. Blockchain brings decentralization, security, and trust to KYC by allowing verified identities to be reused across institutions. A typical KYC blockchain whitepaper outlines frameworks involving shared ledgers, verifiable credentials, user-controlled data, and smart contract automation. Blockchain-based KYC systems offer measurable benefits — including up to 50% faster onboarding and significantly lower compliance costs. Global pilots in regions like Singapore, Dubai, and the EU have already shown strong results in real-world blockchain KYC implementation. Traditional KYC: Challenges and Limitations Legacy KYC systems rely on centralized data collection, manual checks, and siloed processes. Each institution must independently verify the identity of a user, even if that user has been verified elsewhere. This leads to redundant verification, longer onboarding times, and a poor customer experience. Businesses spend heavily on compliance teams, document management, and fraud detection, yet identity fraud remains a growing issue. Here are the main challenges: Centralized Storage : Sensitive user data is stored in centralized databases, which are frequent targets for breaches. Repetitive Checks : Customers must submit the same documents multiple times across platforms. High Compliance Costs : Manual reviews and third-party verifications increase operating costs. Slow Onboarding : Delays in verification can lead to user drop-offs and lost revenue. Limited Transparency : Auditing and proving compliance becomes complex due to fragmented data trails. These issues have led companies to explore more secure and efficient alternatives, one of which is the blockchain technology. Suggested read: Addressing KYC Challenges in Fragmented Identity Verification The Role of Blockchain in KYC Verification Blockchain offers a decentralized way to store, share, and verify identity data. Instead of each institution running isolated KYC checks, they can access a shared, tamper-proof record of verified identities with the user’s consent. At its core, blockchain brings three key advantages to KYC : Immutability : Once data is added to the blockchain, it cannot be altered. This ensures the integrity of verification records. Transparency with Control : Institutions can view verification status without accessing raw documents. Users retain control over what they share and with whom. Automation via Smart Contracts : Predefined rules can trigger automatic KYC validations, making compliance faster and less error-prone. A customer can complete KYC once and share proof of verification with multiple services. These features of blockchain kyc cut costs and shortens onboarding time significantly. These capabilities form the foundation of most frameworks described in a KYC blockchain whitepaper — where trust is distributed, but compliance remains intact. Key Takeaways from a KYC Blockchain Whitepaper Most KYC blockchain insights focus on designing systems that are both secure and compliant. While the specifics vary, several recurring themes and components appear across top whitepapers: Shared Network of Trusted Entities Whitepapers often propose a consortium of verified institutions — such as banks, exchanges, and regulators — that can access and verify identity data from a shared ledger. User-Centric Identity Model The user owns their identity. Through cryptographic keys, they grant or revoke access to their KYC data. This approach ensures privacy and control. Verifiable Credentials Instead of sharing raw documents, users share cryptographic proofs of verification. These can be instantly validated without reprocessing the original data. Smart Contracts for Compliance Automated rules check whether a user meets regulatory requirements before granting access. This reduces manual intervention and ensures consistency. Data Minimization and Consent Only essential information is shared. Access logs are maintained on-chain, allowing real-time auditing and traceability. These frameworks aim to reduce duplication, enhance data security, and build a global standard for interoperable identity verification. Suggested read: What Is KYC In Crypto Security And Compliance, And Why Does It Matter? Benefits of Using Blockchain for KYC Blockchain-based KYC solutions are designed to solve the biggest issues in identity verification: cost, time, and security. As more organizations adopt this model, the benefits are becoming quantifiable. Faster Onboarding Traditional KYC checks can take up to 10 days. With reusable identity data on blockchain, onboarding time can drop by 40% , especially for returning or already-verified users. Lower Compliance Costs Financial institutions spend large sums annually on KYC-related processes. A proof‑of‑concept by KPMG estimated that a blockchain‑enabled KYC utility could deliver cost savings of 25‑50% by reducing duplication and providing a clear audit trail. Improved Data Security Blockchain eliminates the need for centralized storage of personal documents. Identity data is encrypted and shared only with permission, reducing the risk of large-scale breaches. Real-Time Auditing Every access or update on the blockchain is time-stamped and immutable. This creates a live audit trail that improves transparency and simplifies compliance checks. Better User Experience Customers no longer need to upload documents repeatedly. With verified credentials, they can access services faster while staying in control of their data. Pilot programs in financial hubs like Singapore, the UAE, and parts of the EU have already demonstrated strong adoption potential. Governments and institutions in Singapore, Dubai, and parts of the European Union have launched pilot programs to test blockchain-powered KYC. For example, the Monetary Authority of Singapore(MAS) introduced a shared KYC utility ‘ COSMIC ’, allowing banks to access common identity data. In Dubai, the Blockchain KYC Platform connects banks and licensing authorities to reduce onboarding time. These early implementations have shown faster approvals, improved security, and higher user satisfaction. While the benefits are clear today, it’s important to understand how to optimize your kyc for a much faster and efficient verification process. How to Optimize KYC Verification Using Blockchain Blockchain technology doesn&#x27;t just enable a new way to handle KYC — it allows businesses to optimize the entire verification process from onboarding to ongoing compliance. Here are the key strategies to maximize the impact of blockchain in KYC: Eliminate Redundant Checks With reusable credentials, customers can complete KYC once and share verified proofs with multiple institutions. This reduces operational friction and prevents duplicate verification. Automate Compliance Triggers Smart contracts can automate key compliance steps — such as age checks, document expiry alerts, or PEP list screening — allowing teams to shift focus from manual reviews to oversight. Use Permissioned Ledgers for Security and Scalability Public blockchains are not ideal for KYC. Using a permissioned ledger ensures only trusted entities have a ... (truncated if too long)

--- FILE: related_work/README.md ---
# Related Work Collection

## Classification
- On-chain identity/KYC: DID, Verifiable Credentials, ERC-1400, ERC-3643
- Regulation monitoring/reporting: MiCA, FATF RBA for VAs/VASPs, FinCEN Travel Rule, TRISA
- On-chain enforcement primitives: Ethereum Attestation Service (EAS)
- DeFi policy and risk governance: Aave v3, MakerDAO risk framework

## Sources (links)
- W3C, "Decentralized Identifiers (DID) Core 1.0" (2022): https://www.w3.org/TR/did-core/
- W3C, "Verifiable Credentials Data Model 1.1" (2022): https://www.w3.org/TR/vc-data-model/
- Ethereum Foundation, "ERC-1400: Security Token Standard" (2018): https://eips.ethereum.org/EIPS/eip-1400
- Ethereum Foundation, "ERC-3643: T-REX -- Security Token Standard" (2021): https://eips.ethereum.org/EIPS/eip-3643
- Ethereum Attestation Service, "EAS Documentation" (2023): https://docs.attest.sh/
- European Union, "Regulation (EU) 2023/1114 (MiCA)" (2023): https://eur-lex.europa.eu/eli/reg/2023/1114/oj
- FATF, "Guidance for a Risk-Based Approach to Virtual Assets and VASPs" (2019/2021): https://www.fatf-gafi.org/en/publications/Fatfrecommendations/Guidance-RBA-VA-VASPs.html
- FinCEN, "Funds Travel Rule and Funds Transmittal Regulations" (2019): https://www.fincen.gov/resources/statutes-and-regulations/guidance/funds-travel-rule
- TRISA, "Travel Rule Information Sharing Architecture" (2020): https://trisa.io
- Aave Companies, "Aave Protocol V3 Technical Paper" (2022): https://github.com/aave/protocol-v3/blob/master/aave-v3-whitepaper.pdf
- MakerDAO, "The Maker Protocol: MakerDAO's Multi-Collateral Dai (MCD) System" (2017): https://makerdao.com/en/whitepaper

--- END OF FILE ---

--- FILE: related_work/categorization.md ---
# Related Work Categorization for APOL

## Category 1: On-Chain Identity & KYC Standards
### W3C DID Core (did-core.html)
- Decentralized identifier standard for verifiable digital identity
- Enables controller-based identity without centralized registries
- **Gap vs APOL**: DIDs provide identity infrastructure but lack policy enforcement mechanisms

### W3C Verifiable Credentials (vc-data-model.html)
- Privacy-preserving credential model with selective disclosure
- Standardizes claims about subjects with cryptographic verification
- **Gap vs APOL**: VCs handle credential verification but don't enforce on-chain compliance rules

### Ethereum Attestation Service (eas-docs.html)
- On-chain attestation infrastructure for verifiable claims
- Composable attestation schemas with on-chain/off-chain storage
- **Gap vs APOL**: EAS provides attestation primitives but lacks regulatory policy language and enforcement automation

## Category 2: Token-Level Compliance & Regulatory Enforcement
### EIP-3643 T-REX (eip-3643.html)
- Permissioned token standard with compliance rules embedded in smart contracts
- Identity registry + compliance rules for regulated securities
- **Gap vs APOL**: Token-specific compliance vs. APOL's protocol-level policy enforcement across diverse DeFi interactions

### EIP-1400 Security Token Standard (eip-1400.html)
- Partially fungible token standard with document management and transfer restrictions
- Operator-based controls for regulated asset transfers
- **Gap vs APOL**: Focuses on asset tokenization compliance, not general DeFi protocol policy enforcement

## Category 3: DeFi Policy & Risk Management
- **TO COLLECT**: On-chain policy enforcement frameworks, DeFi regulation monitoring systems, automated compliance tools for lending/DEX protocols

## Category 4: Privacy-Preserving Compliance
- **OVERLAP**: W3C VCs, potential ZK-proof-based KYC systems
- **TO COLLECT**: zk-KYC solutions, privacy-preserving regulatory reporting

## Key Insights:
1. **Identity Layer**: W3C standards + EAS provide building blocks APOL can leverage
2. **Token Compliance**: EIP-3643/1400 solve asset-level rules; APOL targets protocol interaction policies
3. **Missing Gap**: No existing work combines expressive policy language + automated enforcement + privacy preservation for general DeFi protocols

--- END OF FILE ---

# Related Work Categorization for APOL

## Category 1: On-Chain Identity & KYC Standards
### W3C DID Core (did-core.html)
- Decentralized identifier standard for verifiable digital identity
- Enables controller-based identity without centralized registries
- **Gap vs APOL**: DIDs provide identity infrastructure but lack policy enforcement mechanisms

### W3C Verifiable Credentials (vc-data-model.html)
- Privacy-preserving credential model with selective disclosure
- Standardizes claims about subjects with cryptographic verification
- **Gap vs APOL**: VCs handle credential verification but don't enforce on-chain compliance rules

### Ethereum Attestation Service (eas-docs.html)
- On-chain attestation infrastructure for verifiable claims
- Composable attestation schemas with on-chain/off-chain storage
- **Gap vs APOL**: EAS provides attestation primitives but lacks regulatory policy language and enforcement automation

## Category 2: Token-Level Compliance & Regulatory Enforcement
### EIP-3643 T-REX (eip-3643.html)
- Permissioned token standard with compliance rules embedded in smart contracts
- Identity registry + compliance rules for regulated securities
- **Gap vs APOL**: Token-specific compliance vs. APOL's protocol-level policy enforcement across diverse DeFi interactions

### EIP-1400 Security Token Standard (eip-1400.html)
- Partially fungible token standard with document management and transfer restrictions
- Operator-based controls for regulated asset transfers
- **Gap vs APOL**: Focuses on asset tokenization compliance, not general DeFi protocol policy enforcement

## Category 3: DeFi Policy & Risk Management
- **TO COLLECT**: On-chain policy enforcement frameworks, DeFi regulation monitoring systems, automated compliance tools for lending/DEX protocols

## Category 4: Privacy-Preserving Compliance
- **OVERLAP**: W3C VCs, potential ZK-proof-based KYC systems
- **TO COLLECT**: zk-KYC solutions, privacy-preserving regulatory reporting

## Key Insights:
1. **Identity Layer**: W3C standards + EAS provide building blocks APOL can leverage
2. **Token Compliance**: EIP-3643/1400 solve asset-level rules; APOL targets protocol interaction policies
3. **Missing Gap**: No existing work combines expressive policy language + automated enforcement + privacy preservation for general DeFi protocols

--- END OF FILE ---

python3 -c "import re, sys; content = open('related_work/websearch_results_raw.txt', 'r', encoding='utf-8', errors='ignore').read(); docs = content.split('---END OF DOCUMENT---'); results = []; 
for i, doc in enumerate(docs[:10]):
    title_match = re.search(r'<title>(.+?)</title>', doc, re.IGNORECASE)
    abstract_match = re.search(r'abstract[^>]*>(.{50,800}?)</[^>]+>', doc, re.IGNORECASE | re.DOTALL)
    h1_match = re.search(r'<h1[^>]*>(.+?)</h1>', doc, re.IGNORECASE)
    meta_desc = re.search(r'<meta[^>]*name=[\"\']description[\"\'][^>]*content=[\"\'](.+?)[\"\']', doc, re.IGNORECASE)
    results.append(f'--- Document {i+1} ---\nTitle: {title_match.group(1) if title_match else \"N/A\"}\nH1: {h1_match.group(1) if h1_match else \"N/A\"}\nMeta Desc: {meta_desc.group(1) if meta_desc else \"N/A\"}\nAbstract Snippet: {abstract_match.group(1)[:300] if abstract_match else \"N/A\"}\n')
open('related_work/parsed_abstracts.txt', 'w', encoding='utf-8').write('\n'.join(results)); print('Parsed abstracts saved to related_work/parsed_abstracts.txt')" && head -n 100 related_work/parsed_abstracts.txt

python3 -c "import requests; from bs4 import BeautifulSoup; urls = open('related_work/related_papers.txt').read().strip().split('\n'); results = []; 
for u in urls:
    try:
        r = requests.get(u, timeout=10, headers={'User-Agent': 'Mozilla/5.0'})
        if r.status_code == 200:
            soup = BeautifulSoup(r.text, 'html.parser')
            title = soup.find('title').get_text(strip=True) if soup.find('title') else 'N/A'
            results.append(f'{u}|||{title}')
        else:
            results.append(f'{u}|||ERROR_{r.status_code}')
    except Exception as e:
        results.append(f'{u}|||EXCEPTION_{str(e)[:50]}')
with open('related_work/url_titles.txt', 'w') as f:
    f.write('\n'.join(results))
print('URL titles extracted to related_work/url_titles.txt')"
